id="content" class="mw-body" role="main">\n\t\t\t<a id="top"></a>\n\t\t\t<div id="siteNotice" class="mw-body-content"><!-- CentralNotice --></div><div class="mw-indicators mw-body-content">\n<div id="mw-indicator-featured-star" class="mw-indicator"><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Featured_articles" title="This is a featured article. 2x" data-file-width="462" data-file-height="438" /></a></div>\n</div>\n<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Ian Fleming</h1>\t\t\t<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">\n\t\t\t\t<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>\t\t\t\t<div id="contentSub"></div>\n\t\t\t\t<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>\t\t\t\t<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>\n\t\t\t\t<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>\n\t\t\t\t<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">For other people named Ian Fleming, see <a href="/wiki/Ian_Fleming_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="Ian Fleming (disambiguation)">Ian Fleming (disambiguation)</a>.</div>\n<p class="mw-empty-elt">\n\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ian_Fleming.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/d/d2/Ian_Fleming.jpg/300px-Ian_Fleming.jpg" width="300" height="282" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/d/d2/Ian_Fleming.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="325" data-file-height="305" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ian_Fleming.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ian Fleming</div></div></div>\n<p><b>Ian Lancaster Fleming</b> (28 May 1908&#160;\xe2\x80\x93 12 August 1964) was an English author, journalist, and naval intelligence officer who is best known for his <a href="/wiki/James_Bond" title="James Bond">James Bond</a> series of spy novels. Fleming came from a wealthy family connected to the merchant bank <a href="/wiki/Robert_Fleming_%26_Co." title="Robert Fleming &amp; Co.">Robert Fleming &amp; Co.</a>, and his father was the Member of Parliament for Henley from 1910 until his death on the <a href="/wiki/Western_Front_(World_War_I)" title="Western Front (World War I)">Western Front</a> in 1917. He was educated at <a href="/wiki/Eton_College" title="Eton College">Eton</a>, <a href="/wiki/Royal_Military_College,_Sandhurst" title="Royal Military College, Sandhurst">Sandhurst</a>, and the universities of <a href="/wiki/Munich_University" class="mw-redirect" title="Munich University">Munich</a> and <a href="/wiki/University_of_Geneva" title="University of Geneva">Geneva</a>, and he moved through several jobs before he started writing.\n</p><p>Fleming worked for Britain\'s <a href="/wiki/Naval_Intelligence_Division" title="Naval Intelligence Division">Naval Intelligence Division</a> during the Second World War and was involved in planning <a href="/wiki/Operation_Goldeneye" title="Operation Goldeneye">Operation Goldeneye</a> and in the planning and oversight of the <a href="/wiki/30_Assault_Unit" class="mw-redirect" title="30 Assault Unit">30 Assault Unit</a> and <a href="/wiki/T-Force" title="T-Force">T-Force</a> intelligence units. His wartime service and his career as a journalist provided much of the background, detail, and depth of the James Bond novels.\n</p><p>Fleming wrote his first Bond novel <i><a href="/wiki/Casino_Royale_(novel)" title="Casino Royale (novel)">Casino Royale</a></i> in 1952. It was a success, with three print runs being commissioned to cope with the demand. Eleven Bond novels and two collections of short stories followed between 1953 and 1966. The novels revolved around James Bond, an officer in the <a href="/wiki/Secret_Intelligence_Service" title="Secret Intelligence Service">Secret Intelligence Service</a> commonly known as MI6. Bond was also known by his code number 007, and was a commander in the Royal Naval Reserve. The Bond stories rank among the best-selling series of fictional books of all time, having sold more than 100 million copies worldwide. Fleming also wrote the children\'s story <i><a href="/wiki/Chitty-Chitty-Bang-Bang" title="Chitty-Chitty-Bang-Bang">Chitty-Chitty-Bang-Bang</a></i> and two works of non-fiction. In 2008, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Times" title="The Times">The Times</a></i> ranked Fleming 14th on its list of "The 50 greatest British writers since 1945".\n</p><p>Fleming was married to <a href="/wiki/Ann_Fleming" title="Ann Fleming">Ann Charteris</a>, who was divorced from the second <a href="/wiki/Esmond_Harmsworth,_2nd_Viscount_Rothermere" title="Esmond Harmsworth, 2nd Viscount Rothermere">Viscount Rothermere</a> because of her affair with Fleming. The couple had a son named Caspar. Fleming was a heavy smoker and drinker for most of his life and succumbed to heart disease in 1964 at age 56. Two of his James Bond books were published posthumously. Fleming\'s creation has appeared in film 26 times, portrayed by seven actors.\n</p>\n<div class="toclimit-3"><div id="toc" class="toc"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Biography">Biography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ian_Fleming&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Biography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Birth_and_family">Birth and family</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ian_Fleming&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Birth and family">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:A_link_to_James_Bond_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1255756.jpg" class="image"><img alt="a discoloured brass plaque showing the names of those local men killed in the First World War" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/A_link_to_James_Bond_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1255756.jpg/220px-A_link_to_James_Bond_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1255756.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/A_link_to_James_Bond_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1255756.jpg/330px-A_link_to_James_Bond_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1255756.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/A_link_to_James_Bond_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1255756.jpg/440px-A_link_to_James_Bond_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1255756.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="480" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:A_link_to_James_Bond_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1255756.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Glenelg,_Highland" title="Glenelg, Highland">Glenelg</a> War Memorial, listing <a href="/wiki/Valentine_Fleming" title="Valentine Fleming">Valentine Fleming</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Ian Lancaster Fleming was born on 28&#160;May 1908, at 27&#160;<a href="/wiki/Green_Street,_Mayfair" title="Green Street, Mayfair">Green Street</a> in the wealthy London district of <a href="/wiki/Mayfair" title="Mayfair">Mayfair</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Lycett_(DNB)_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lycett_(DNB)-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> His mother was <a href="/wiki/Evelyn_St._Croix_Fleming" title="Evelyn St. Croix Fleming">Evelyn</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/Given_name#Name_at_birth" title="Given name">n\xc3\xa9e</a></i> Rose), and his father was <a href="/wiki/Valentine_Fleming" title="Valentine Fleming">Valentine Fleming</a>, the Member of Parliament for <a href="/wiki/Henley_(UK_Parliament_constituency)" title="Henley (UK Parliament constituency)">Henley</a> from 1910.<sup id="cite_ref-Churchill_Obit_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Churchill_Obit-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> As an infant he briefly lived, with his family, at <a href="/wiki/Braziers_Park" title="Braziers Park">Braziers Park</a> in Oxfordshire.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> Fleming was a grandson of the Scottish financier <a href="/wiki/Robert_Fleming_(financier)" title="Robert Fleming (financier)">Robert Fleming</a>, who founded the Scottish American Investment Trust and the merchant bank <a href="/wiki/Robert_Fleming_%26_Co." title="Robert Fleming &amp; Co.">Robert Fleming &amp; Co.</a><sup id="cite_ref-Lycett_(DNB)_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lycett_(DNB)-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;a&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1914, with the start of the First World War, <a href="/wiki/Valentine_Fleming" title="Valentine Fleming">Valentine Fleming</a> joined <a href="/wiki/Squadron_(army)" title="Squadron (army)">"C" Squadron</a>, <a href="/wiki/Queen%27s_Own_Oxfordshire_Hussars" title="Queen&#39;s Own Oxfordshire Hussars">Queen\'s Own Oxfordshire Hussars</a>, and rose to the rank of <a href="/wiki/Major_(United_Kingdom)" title="Major (United Kingdom)">major</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Churchill_Obit_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Churchill_Obit-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> He was killed by German shelling on the <a href="/wiki/Western_Front_(World_War_I)" title="Western Front (World War I)">Western Front</a> on 20&#160;May 1917; <a href="/wiki/Winston_Churchill" title="Winston Churchill">Winston Churchill</a> wrote an obituary that appeared in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Times" title="The Times">The Times</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELycett199612_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELycett199612-7">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> Because the family owned an estate at <a href="/wiki/Arnisdale" title="Arnisdale">Arnisdale</a>, Valentine\'s death was commemorated on the <a href="/wiki/Glenelg,_Highland" title="Glenelg, Highland">Glenelg</a> War Memorial.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELycett199613_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELycett199613-8">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Fleming\'s elder brother <a href="/wiki/Peter_Fleming_(writer)" title="Peter Fleming (writer)">Peter</a> (1907\xe2\x80\x931971) became a travel writer and married actress <a href="/wiki/Celia_Johnson" title="Celia Johnson">Celia Johnson</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-PF_Obit_(1971)_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PF_Obit_(1971)-9">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> Peter served with the <a href="/wiki/Grenadier_Guards" title="Grenadier Guards">Grenadier Guards</a> during the Second World War, was later commissioned under <a href="/wiki/Colin_Gubbins" title="Colin Gubbins">Colin Gubbins</a> to help establish the <a href="/wiki/Auxiliary_Units" title="Auxiliary Units">Auxiliary Units</a>, and became involved in behind-the-lines operations in Norway and Greece during the war.<sup id="cite_ref-PF_Obit_(1971)_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PF_Obit_(1971)-9">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Fleming also had two younger brothers, Michael (1913\xe2\x80\x931940) and Richard (1911\xe2\x80\x931977), and a younger maternal half-sister born out of wedlock, the cellist <a href="/wiki/Amaryllis_Fleming" title="Amaryllis Fleming">Amaryllis Fleming</a> (1925\xe2\x80\x931999), whose father was the artist <a href="/wiki/Augustus_John" title="Augustus John">Augustus John</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> Amaryllis was conceived during a long-term affair between John and Evelyn that started in 1923, six years after the death of Valentine.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELycett199619_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELycett199619-11">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Education_and_early_life">Education and early life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ian_Fleming&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Education and early life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>In 1914 Fleming attended <a href="/wiki/Durnford_School" title="Durnford School">Durnford School</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Preparatory_school_(UK)" class="mw-redirect" title="Preparatory school (UK)">preparatory school</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Isle_of_Purbeck" title="Isle of Purbeck">Isle of Purbeck</a> in <a href="/wiki/Dorset" title="Dorset">Dorset</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDelFattore198986_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDelFattore198986-12">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;b&#93;</a></sup> He did not enjoy his time at Durnford; he suffered unpalatable food, physical hardship and bullying.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDelFattore198986_12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDelFattore198986-12">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Eton_College_quadrangle.jpg" class="image"><img alt="A building in the Tudor style with a courtyard in front" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Eton_College_quadrangle.jpg/220px-Eton_College_quadrangle.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Eton_College_quadrangle.jpg/330px-Eton_College_quadrangle.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Eton_College_quadrangle.jpg/440px-Eton_College_quadrangle.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3456" data-file-height="2304" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Eton_College_quadrangle.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Eton_College" title="Eton College">Eton College</a>: Fleming\'s <i>alma mater</i> from 1921 to 1927</div></div></div>\n<p>In 1921 Fleming enrolled at <a href="/wiki/Eton_College" title="Eton College">Eton College</a>. Not a high achiever academically, he excelled at athletics and held the title of <i><a href="/wiki/Victor_Ludorum" class="mw-redirect" title="Victor Ludorum">Victor Ludorum</a></i> ("Winner of the Games") for two years between 1925 and 1927.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacintyre200833_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacintyre200833-15">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> He also edited a school magazine, <i>The Wyvern</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Lycett_(DNB)_1-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lycett_(DNB)-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> His lifestyle at Eton brought him into conflict with his housemaster, E. V. Slater, who disapproved of Fleming\'s attitude, his hair oil, his ownership of a car and his relations with women.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDelFattore198986_12-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDelFattore198986-12">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> Slater persuaded Fleming\'s mother to remove him from Eton a term early for a <a href="/wiki/Cram_school" title="Cram school">crammer</a> course to gain entry to the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Military_College,_Sandhurst" title="Royal Military College, Sandhurst">Royal Military College</a> at Sandhurst.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDelFattore198986_12-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDelFattore198986-12">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> He spent less than a year there, leaving in 1927 without gaining a commission, after contracting <a href="/wiki/Gonorrhea" title="Gonorrhea">gonorrhea</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacintyre200833_15-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacintyre200833-15">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1927, to prepare Fleming for possible entry into the <a href="/wiki/Foreign_Office" class="mw-redirect" title="Foreign Office">Foreign Office</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenson198845_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenson198845-16">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> his mother sent him to the Tennerhof in <a href="/wiki/Kitzb%C3%BChel" title="Kitzb\xc3\xbchel">Kitzb\xc3\xbchel</a>, Austria, a small private school run by the <a href="/wiki/Adlerian" title="Adlerian">Adlerian</a> disciple and former British spy Ernan Forbes Dennis and his novelist wife, <a href="/wiki/Phyllis_Bottome" title="Phyllis Bottome">Phyllis Bottome</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDelFattore198987_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDelFattore198987-17">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> After improving his language skills there, he studied briefly at <a href="/wiki/Munich_University" class="mw-redirect" title="Munich University">Munich University</a> and the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Geneva" title="University of Geneva">University of Geneva</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Lycett_(DNB)_1-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lycett_(DNB)-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> While in Geneva, Fleming began a romance with Monique Panchaud de Bottens and the couple were briefly engaged in 1931.<sup id="cite_ref-S_Tel&#58;_Bottens_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S_Tel:_Bottens-18">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;c&#93;</a></sup> His mother disapproved and made him break off the relationship.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacintyre200834_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacintyre200834-21">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> He applied for entry to the Foreign Office, but failed the examinations. His mother again intervened in his affairs, lobbying Sir Roderick Jones, head of <a href="/wiki/Reuters" title="Reuters">Reuters</a> News Agency, and in October 1931 he was given a position as a <a href="/wiki/Sub-editor" class="mw-redirect" title="Sub-editor">sub-editor</a> and journalist for the company.<sup id="cite_ref-Lycett_(DNB)_1-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lycett_(DNB)-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> In 1933 Fleming spent time in Moscow, where he covered the <a href="/wiki/Stalinism" title="Stalinism">Stalinist</a> <a href="/wiki/Show_trial" title="Show trial">show trial</a> of six engineers from the British company <a href="/wiki/Metropolitan-Vickers" title="Metropolitan-Vickers">Metropolitan-Vickers</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenson198846_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenson198846-22">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> While there he applied for an interview with Soviet premier <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Stalin" title="Joseph Stalin">Joseph Stalin</a>, and was amazed to receive a personally signed note apologising for not being able to attend.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacintyre200839_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacintyre200839-23">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Fleming bowed to family pressure in October 1933, and went into banking with a position at the financiers Cull &amp; Co.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenson198846_22-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenson198846-22">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> In 1935 he moved to Rowe and Pitman on <a href="/wiki/Bishopsgate" title="Bishopsgate">Bishopsgate</a> as a stockbroker.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacintyre200839_23-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacintyre200839-23">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> Fleming was unsuccessful in both roles.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELycett199672_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELycett199672-24">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenson198846_22-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenson198846-22">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> Early in 1939 Fleming began an affair with Ann O\'Neill (n\xc3\xa9e Charteris), who was married to the <a href="/wiki/Baron_O%27Neill" title="Baron O&#39;Neill">3rd Baron O\'Neill</a>;<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELycett199696_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELycett199696-25">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> she was also having an affair with <a href="/wiki/Esmond_Harmsworth,_2nd_Viscount_Rothermere" title="Esmond Harmsworth, 2nd Viscount Rothermere">Esmond Harmsworth</a>, the heir to Lord Rothermere, owner of the <i>Daily Mail</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Lycett_(Ann_DNB)_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lycett_(Ann_DNB)-26">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Second_World_War">Second World War</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ian_Fleming&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Second World War">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Old_Admiralty_Building_2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="A red brick building with white stone detailing in the Queen Anne style with French influences" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Old_Admiralty_Building_2.jpg/170px-Old_Admiralty_Building_2.jpg" width="170" height="256" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Old_Admiralty_Building_2.jpg/255px-Old_Admiralty_Building_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Old_Admiralty_Building_2.jpg/340px-Old_Admiralty_Building_2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2000" data-file-height="3008" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Old_Admiralty_Building_2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Admiralty#The_Admiralty" title="Admiralty">The Admiralty</a>, where Fleming worked in the <a href="/wiki/Naval_Intelligence_Division" title="Naval Intelligence Division">Naval Intelligence Division</a> during the Second World War</div></div></div>\n<p>In May 1939 Fleming was recruited by <a href="/wiki/Rear_Admiral" class="mw-redirect" title="Rear Admiral">Rear Admiral</a> <a href="/wiki/John_Henry_Godfrey" title="John Henry Godfrey">John Godfrey</a>, <a href="/wiki/Naval_Intelligence_Division" title="Naval Intelligence Division">Director of Naval Intelligence</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Navy" title="Royal Navy">Royal Navy</a>, to become his <a href="/wiki/Personal_assistant" title="Personal assistant">personal assistant</a>. He joined the organisation full-time in August 1939,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELycett199699_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELycett199699-27">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> with the codename "17F",<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGant196645_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGant196645-28">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> and worked out of Room 39 at <a href="/wiki/Ripley_Building" class="mw-redirect" title="Ripley Building">The Admiralty</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELycett1996101_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELycett1996101-29">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> Fleming\'s biographer, <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Lycett" title="Andrew Lycett">Andrew Lycett</a>, notes that Fleming had "no obvious qualifications" for the role.<sup id="cite_ref-Lycett_(DNB)_1-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lycett_(DNB)-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> As part of his appointment, Fleming was commissioned into the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Naval_Volunteer_Reserve" class="mw-redirect" title="Royal Naval Volunteer Reserve">Royal Naval Volunteer Reserve</a> in July 1939,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELycett199699_27-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELycett199699-27">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> initially as <a href="/wiki/Lieutenant_(navy)" title="Lieutenant (navy)">lieutenant</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELycett1996101_29-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELycett1996101-29">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> but promoted to <a href="/wiki/Commander" title="Commander">commander</a> a few months later.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELycett1996103_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELycett1996103-30">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Fleming proved invaluable as Godfrey\'s personal assistant and excelled in administration.<sup id="cite_ref-Lycett_(DNB)_1-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lycett_(DNB)-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> Godfrey was known as an abrasive character who made enemies within government circles. He frequently used Fleming as a liaison with other sections of the government\'s wartime administration, such as the <a href="/wiki/Secret_Intelligence_Service" title="Secret Intelligence Service">Secret Intelligence Service</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Political_Warfare_Executive" title="Political Warfare Executive">Political Warfare Executive</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Special_Operations_Executive" title="Special Operations Executive">Special Operations Executive</a> (SOE), the <a href="/wiki/Joint_Intelligence_Committee_(United_Kingdom)" title="Joint Intelligence Committee (United Kingdom)">Joint Intelligence Committee</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Prime Minister of the United Kingdom">Prime Minister</a>\'s staff.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChancellor200528_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChancellor200528-31">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On 29 September 1939, soon after the start of the war, Godfrey circulated a memorandum that, "bore all the hallmarks of&#160;... Lieutenant Commander Ian Fleming", according to historian <a href="/wiki/Ben_Macintyre" title="Ben Macintyre">Ben Macintyre</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacintyre20106_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacintyre20106-32">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> It was called the <a href="/wiki/Trout_Memo" class="mw-redirect" title="Trout Memo">Trout Memo</a> and compared the deception of an enemy in wartime to <a href="/wiki/Fly_fishing" title="Fly fishing">fly fishing</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacintyre20106_32-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacintyre20106-32">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> The memo contained several schemes to be considered for use against the <a href="/wiki/Axis_powers" title="Axis powers">Axis powers</a> to lure <a href="/wiki/U-boats" class="mw-redirect" title="U-boats">U-boats</a> and German surface ships towards minefields.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChancellor200529_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChancellor200529-33">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> Number 28 on the list was an idea to plant misleading papers on a corpse that would be found by the enemy; the suggestion is similar to <a href="/wiki/Operation_Mincemeat" title="Operation Mincemeat">Operation Mincemeat</a>, the 1943 plan to conceal the <a href="/wiki/Operation_Husky" class="mw-redirect" title="Operation Husky">intended invasion of Italy</a> from North Africa, which was developed by Charles Cholmondoley in October 1942.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacintyre20107_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacintyre20107-34">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> The recommendation in the Trout Memo was titled: "A Suggestion (not a very nice one)",<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacintyre20107_34-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacintyre20107-34">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> and continued: "The following suggestion is used in a book by <a href="/wiki/Basil_Thomson" title="Basil Thomson">Basil Thomson</a>: a corpse dressed as an airman, with despatches in his pockets, could be dropped on the coast, supposedly from a parachute that has failed. I understand there is no difficulty in obtaining corpses at the Naval Hospital, but, of course, it would have to be a fresh one."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacintyre20107_34-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacintyre20107-34">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1940 Fleming and Godfrey contacted <a href="/wiki/Kenneth_Mason_(geographer)" title="Kenneth Mason (geographer)">Kenneth Mason</a>, Professor of Geography at <a href="/wiki/University_of_Oxford" title="University of Oxford">Oxford University</a>, about the preparation of reports on the geography of countries involved in military operations. These reports were the precursors of the <a href="/wiki/Naval_Intelligence_Handbooks" title="Naval Intelligence Handbooks"><i>Naval Intelligence Division Geographical Handbook Series</i></a> produced between 1941 and 1946.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Operation_Ruthless" title="Operation Ruthless">Operation Ruthless</a>, a plan aimed at obtaining details of the <a href="/wiki/Cryptanalysis_of_the_Enigma#German_Navy_3-rotor_Enigma" title="Cryptanalysis of the Enigma">Enigma codes</a> used by <a href="/wiki/Kriegsmarine" title="Kriegsmarine">Nazi Germany\'s navy</a>, was instigated by a memo written by Fleming to Godfrey on 12 September 1940. The idea was to "obtain" a German bomber, man it with a German-speaking crew dressed in Luftwaffe uniforms, and crash it into the English Channel. The crew would then attack their German rescuers and bring their boat and <a href="/wiki/Enigma_machine" title="Enigma machine">Enigma machine</a> back to England.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELycett1996121_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELycett1996121-36">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> Much to the annoyance of <a href="/wiki/Alan_Turing" title="Alan Turing">Alan Turing</a> and <a href="/wiki/Peter_Twinn" title="Peter Twinn">Peter Twinn</a> at <a href="/wiki/Bletchley_Park" title="Bletchley Park">Bletchley Park</a>, the mission was never carried out. According to Fleming\'s niece, <a href="/wiki/Lucy_Fleming" title="Lucy Fleming">Lucy</a>, an official of the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Air_Force" title="Royal Air Force">Royal Air Force</a> pointed out that if they were to drop a downed <a href="/wiki/Heinkel" title="Heinkel">Heinkel</a> bomber in the English Channel, it would probably sink rather quickly.<sup id="cite_ref-Correspondence_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Correspondence-37">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Fleming also worked with Colonel <a href="/wiki/William_Joseph_Donovan" class="mw-redirect" title="William Joseph Donovan">"Wild Bill" Donovan</a>, President <a href="/wiki/Franklin_D._Roosevelt" title="Franklin D. Roosevelt">Franklin D. Roosevelt</a>\'s special representative on intelligence co-operation between London and Washington.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELycett1996120_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELycett1996120-38">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> In May 1941 Fleming accompanied Godfrey to the United States, where he assisted in writing a <a href="/wiki/Blueprint" title="Blueprint">blueprint</a> for the <a href="/wiki/Office_of_the_Coordinator_of_Information" title="Office of the Coordinator of Information">Office of the Coordinator of Information</a>, the department that turned into the <a href="/wiki/Office_of_Strategic_Services" title="Office of Strategic Services">Office of Strategic Services</a> and eventually became the <a href="/wiki/Central_Intelligence_Agency" title="Central Intelligence Agency">CIA</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPearson1967137_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPearson1967137-39">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Admiral Godfrey put Fleming in charge of <a href="/wiki/Operation_Goldeneye" title="Operation Goldeneye">Operation Goldeneye</a> between 1941 and 1942; Goldeneye was a plan to maintain an intelligence framework in Spain in the event of a German takeover of the territory.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELycett1996124\xe2\x80\x93125_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELycett1996124\xe2\x80\x93125-40">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> Fleming\'s plan involved maintaining communication with <a href="/wiki/Gibraltar" title="Gibraltar">Gibraltar</a> and launching sabotage operations against the Nazis.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacintyre200854_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacintyre200854-41">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> In 1941 he liaised with Donovan over American involvement in a measure intended to ensure that the Germans did not dominate the seaways.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELycett1996125_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELycett1996125-42">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="30_Assault_Unit">30 Assault Unit</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ian_Fleming&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: 30 Assault Unit">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>In 1942 Fleming formed a unit of <a href="/wiki/British_Commandos" class="mw-redirect" title="British Commandos">commandos</a>, known as <a href="/wiki/No._30_Commando" title="No. 30 Commando">No. 30 Commando</a> or 30 Assault Unit (30AU), composed of specialist intelligence troops.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERankin2011136_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERankin2011136-43">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> 30AU\'s job was to be near the front line of an advance\xe2\x80\x94sometimes in front of it\xe2\x80\x94to seize enemy documents from previously targeted headquarters.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELongden20102_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELongden20102-44">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> The unit was based on a German group headed by <a href="/wiki/Otto_Skorzeny" title="Otto Skorzeny">Otto Skorzeny</a>, who had undertaken similar activities in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Crete" title="Battle of Crete">Battle of Crete</a> in May 1941.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChancellor200533_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChancellor200533-45">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> The German unit was thought by Fleming to be "one of the most outstanding innovations in German intelligence".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacintyre200850_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacintyre200850-46">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Fleming did not fight in the field with the unit, but selected targets and directed operations from the rear.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChancellor200533_45-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChancellor200533-45">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> On its formation the unit was thirty strong, but it grew to five times that size.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacintyre200850_46-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacintyre200850-46">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> The unit was filled with men from other commando units, and trained in unarmed combat, safe-cracking and lock-picking at the SOE facilities.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChancellor200533_45-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChancellor200533-45">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> In late 1942 <a href="/wiki/Captain_(naval)" title="Captain (naval)">Captain</a> (later <a href="/wiki/Rear-Admiral_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Rear-Admiral of the United Kingdom">Rear-Admiral</a>) Edmund Rushbrooke replaced Godfrey as head of the Naval Intelligence Division, and Fleming\'s influence in the organisation declined, although he retained control over 30AU.<sup id="cite_ref-Lycett_(DNB)_1-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lycett_(DNB)-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> Fleming was unpopular with the unit\'s members,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacintyre200850_46-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacintyre200850-46">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> who disliked his referring to them as his "Red Indians".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELycett1996152_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELycett1996152-47">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Before the 1944 <a href="/wiki/Normandy_landings" title="Normandy landings">Normandy landings</a>, most of 30AU\'s operations were in the Mediterranean, and it also secretly participated in the <a href="/wiki/Dieppe_Raid" title="Dieppe Raid">Dieppe Raid</a> in a failed pinch raid for an Enigma machine and related materials. Fleming observed the raid from <a href="/wiki/HMS_Fernie_(L11)" title="HMS Fernie (L11)">HMS <i>Fernie</i></a>, 700 yards offshore.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> Because of its successes in Sicily and Italy, 30AU became greatly trusted by naval intelligence.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERankin2011220_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERankin2011220-49">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELongden20106_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELongden20106-50">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In March 1944 Fleming oversaw the distribution of intelligence to Royal Navy units in preparation for <a href="/wiki/Operation_Overlord" title="Operation Overlord">Operation Overlord</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacintyre200856_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacintyre200856-51">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> He was replaced as head of 30AU on 6 June 1944,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChancellor200533_45-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChancellor200533-45">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> but maintained some involvement.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELycett1996152\xe2\x80\x933_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELycett1996152\xe2\x80\x933-52">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> He visited 30AU in the field during and after Overlord, especially following an attack on <a href="/wiki/Cherbourg" class="mw-redirect" title="Cherbourg">Cherbourg</a> for which he was concerned that the unit had been incorrectly used as a regular commando force rather than an intelligence-gathering unit. This wasted the men\'s specialist skills, risked their safety on operations that did not justify the use of such skilled operatives, and threatened the vital gathering of intelligence. Afterwards, the management of these units was revised.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERankin2011220_49-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERankin2011220-49">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> He also followed the unit into Germany after it located, in Tambach Castle, the German naval archives from 1870.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacintyre200856\xe2\x80\x9357_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacintyre200856\xe2\x80\x9357-53">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In December 1944 Fleming was posted on an intelligence fact-finding trip to the Far East on behalf of the Director of Naval Intelligence.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELycett1996154_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELycett1996154-54">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> Much of the trip was spent identifying opportunities for 30AU in the Pacific;<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELycett1996154\xe2\x80\x93155_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELycett1996154\xe2\x80\x93155-55">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> the unit saw little action because of the <a href="/wiki/Surrender_of_Japan" title="Surrender of Japan">Japanese surrender</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="T-Force">T-Force</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ian_Fleming&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: T-Force">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:GoldeneyeEstate.jpg" class="image"><img alt="A white-washed bungalow with a lawn in front" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/GoldeneyeEstate.jpg/220px-GoldeneyeEstate.jpg" width="220" height="141" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/GoldeneyeEstate.jpg/330px-GoldeneyeEstate.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/GoldeneyeEstate.jpg/440px-GoldeneyeEstate.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1022" data-file-height="654" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:GoldeneyeEstate.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Goldeneye_(estate)" title="Goldeneye (estate)">Goldeneye</a>, where Fleming wrote all the Bond stories</div></div></div>\n<p>The success of 30AU led to the August 1944 decision to establish a "Target Force", which became known as <a href="/wiki/T-Force" title="T-Force">T-Force</a>. The official memorandum, held at <a href="/wiki/The_National_Archives_(United_Kingdom)" title="The National Archives (United Kingdom)">The National Archives</a> in London, describes the unit\'s primary role: "T-Force = Target Force, to guard and secure documents, persons, equipment, with combat and Intelligence personnel, after capture of large towns, ports etc. in liberated and enemy territory."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELongden201045_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELongden201045-57">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Fleming sat on the committee that selected the targets for the T-Force unit, and listed them in the "Black Books" that were issued to the unit\'s officers.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELongden201051_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELongden201051-58">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> The infantry component of T-Force was in part made up of the 5th Battalion, <a href="/wiki/King%27s_Regiment" title="King&#39;s Regiment">King\'s Regiment</a>, which supported the <a href="/wiki/Second_Army_(United_Kingdom)" title="Second Army (United Kingdom)">Second Army</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELongden201078_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELongden201078-59">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> It was responsible for securing targets of interest for the British military, including nuclear laboratories, gas research centres and individual rocket scientists. The unit\'s most notable discoveries came during the advance on the German port of <a href="/wiki/Kiel" title="Kiel">Kiel</a>, in the research centre for German engines used in the <a href="/wiki/V-2_rocket" title="V-2 rocket">V-2 rocket</a>, <a href="/wiki/Messerschmitt_Me_163" class="mw-redirect" title="Messerschmitt Me 163">Messerschmitt Me 163</a> fighters and high-speed U-boats.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELongden2010198_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELongden2010198-60">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> Fleming would later use elements of the activities of T-Force in his writing, particularly in his 1955 Bond novel <i><a href="/wiki/Moonraker_(novel)" title="Moonraker (novel)">Moonraker</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELongden2010377_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELongden2010377-61">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1942 Fleming attended an Anglo-American intelligence summit in Jamaica and, despite the constant heavy rain during his visit, he decided to live on the island once the war was over.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGant196651_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGant196651-62">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> His friend Ivar Bryce helped find a plot of land in <a href="/wiki/Saint_Mary_Parish,_Jamaica" title="Saint Mary Parish, Jamaica">Saint Mary Parish</a> where, in 1945, Fleming had a house built, which he named <a href="/wiki/Goldeneye_(estate)" title="Goldeneye (estate)">Goldeneye</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPearson1967161_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPearson1967161-63">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> The name of the house and estate where he wrote his novels has many possible sources. Fleming himself mentioned both his wartime Operation Goldeneye<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELycett1996165_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELycett1996165-64">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Carson_McCullers" title="Carson McCullers">Carson McCullers</a>\' 1941 novel <i><a href="/wiki/Reflections_in_a_Golden_Eye_(novel)" title="Reflections in a Golden Eye (novel)">Reflections in a Golden Eye</a></i>, which described the use of British naval bases in the Caribbean by the American navy.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPearson1967161_63-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPearson1967161-63">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Fleming was awarded the Danish Frihedsmedalje in October 1947 for his contribution in assisting Danish officers escaping from Denmark to Britain during the occupation of Denmark.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Post-war">Post-war</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ian_Fleming&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Post-war">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Upon Fleming\'s <a href="/wiki/Demobilisation_of_the_British_Armed_Forces_after_the_Second_World_War" title="Demobilisation of the British Armed Forces after the Second World War">demobilisation</a> in May 1945, he became the Foreign Manager in the Kemsley newspaper group, which at the time owned <i><a href="/wiki/The_Sunday_Times" title="The Sunday Times">The Sunday Times</a></i>. In this role he oversaw the paper\'s worldwide network of correspondents. His contract allowed him to take three months holiday every winter, which he took in Jamaica.<sup id="cite_ref-Lycett_(DNB)_1-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lycett_(DNB)-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> Fleming worked full-time for the paper until December 1959,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELycett1996360\xe2\x80\x93361_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELycett1996360\xe2\x80\x93361-66">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> but continued to write articles and attend the Tuesday weekly meetings until at least 1961.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELycett1996384_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELycett1996384-67">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELycett1996394_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELycett1996394-68">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>After Ann Charteris\' first husband died in the war, she expected to marry Fleming, but he decided to remain a bachelor.<sup id="cite_ref-Lycett_(DNB)_1-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lycett_(DNB)-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> On 28 June 1945, she married the <a href="/wiki/Esmond_Harmsworth,_2nd_Viscount_Rothermere" title="Esmond Harmsworth, 2nd Viscount Rothermere">second Viscount Rothermere</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Lycett_(Ann_DNB)_26-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lycett_(Ann_DNB)-26">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> Nevertheless, Charteris continued her affair with Fleming, travelling to Jamaica to see him under the pretext of visiting his friend and neighbour <a href="/wiki/No%C3%ABl_Coward" title="No\xc3\xabl Coward">No\xc3\xabl Coward</a>. In 1948 she gave birth to Fleming\'s daughter, Mary, who was <a href="/wiki/Stillbirth" title="Stillbirth">stillborn</a>. Rothermere divorced Charteris in 1951 because of her relationship with Fleming,<sup id="cite_ref-Lycett_(Ann_DNB)_26-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lycett_(Ann_DNB)-26">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> and the couple married on 24 March 1952 in Jamaica,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELycett1996217_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELycett1996217-69">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> a few months before their son Caspar was born in August. Both Fleming and Ann had affairs during their marriage, she with <a href="/wiki/Hugh_Gaitskell" title="Hugh Gaitskell">Hugh Gaitskell</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Leader_of_the_Labour_Party_(UK)" title="Leader of the Labour Party (UK)">Leader of the Labour Party</a> and <a href="/wiki/Leader_of_the_Opposition_(United_Kingdom)" title="Leader of the Opposition (United Kingdom)">Leader of the Opposition</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELycett1996295_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELycett1996295-70">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> Fleming had a long-term affair in Jamaica with one of his neighbours, <a href="/wiki/Blanche_Blackwell" title="Blanche Blackwell">Blanche Blackwell</a>, the mother of <a href="/wiki/Chris_Blackwell" title="Chris Blackwell">Chris Blackwell</a> of <a href="/wiki/Island_Records" title="Island Records">Island Records</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChancellor2005113_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChancellor2005113-71">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1950s">1950s</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ian_Fleming&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: 1950s">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="quotebox pullquote floatright" style="width:200px;&#32;;"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303512">.mw-parser-output .quotebox{background-color:#F9F9F9;border:1px solid #aaa;box-sizing:border-box;padding:10px;font-size:88%}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft{margin:0.5em 1.4em 0.8em 0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright{margin:0.5em 0 0.8em 1.4em}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.centered{margin:0.5em auto 0.8em auto}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft p,.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright p{font-style:inherit}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-title{background-color:#F9F9F9;text-align:center;font-size:larger;font-weight:bold}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:before{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" \xe2\x80\x9c ";vertical-align:-45%;line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:after{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" \xe2\x80\x9d ";line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .left-aligned{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .right-aligned{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .center-aligned{text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .quotebox cite{display:block;font-style:normal}@media screen and (max-width:360px){.mw-parser-output .quotebox{min-width:100%;margin:0 0 0.8em!important;float:none!important}}</style>\n<div class="quotebox-quote left-aligned" style="">The scent and smoke and sweat of a casino are nauseating at three in the morning. Then the soul erosion produced by high gambling \xe2\x80\x94 a compost of greed and fear and nervous tension \xe2\x80\x94 becomes unbearable and the senses awake and revolt from it.</div>\n<p><cite class="right-aligned" style="">Opening lines of <a href="/wiki/Casino_Royale_(novel)" title="Casino Royale (novel)"><i>Casino Royale</i></a></cite>\n</p>\n</div>\n<p>Fleming had first mentioned to friends during the war that he wanted to write a spy novel,<sup id="cite_ref-Lycett_(DNB)_1-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lycett_(DNB)-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> an ambition he achieved within two months with <a href="/wiki/Casino_Royale_(novel)" title="Casino Royale (novel)"><i>Casino Royale</i></a>.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> He started writing the book at Goldeneye on 17 February 1952, gaining inspiration from his own experiences and imagination. He claimed afterwards that he wrote the novel to distract himself from his forthcoming wedding to the pregnant Charteris,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBennettWoollacott20031_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBennettWoollacott20031-73">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> and called the work his "dreadful oafish opus".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacintyre200819_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacintyre200819-74">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> His manuscript was typed in London by Joan Howe (mother of travel writer <a href="/wiki/Rory_MacLean" title="Rory MacLean">Rory MacLean</a>), and Fleming\'s red-haired secretary at <i>The Times</i> on whom the character <a href="/wiki/Miss_Moneypenny" title="Miss Moneypenny">Miss Moneypenny</a> was partially based.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacLean201257_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacLean201257-75">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> Clare Blanchard, a former girlfriend, advised him not to publish the book, or at least to do so under a pseudonym.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChancellor20055_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChancellor20055-76">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>During <i>Casino Royale\'s</i> final draft stages, Fleming allowed his friend <a href="/wiki/William_Plomer" title="William Plomer">William Plomer</a> to see a copy, and remarked "so far as I can see the element of suspense is completely absent".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELycett1996226_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELycett1996226-77">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> Despite this, Plomer thought the book had sufficient promise and sent a copy to the publishing house <a href="/wiki/Jonathan_Cape" title="Jonathan Cape">Jonathan Cape</a>. At first, they were unenthusiastic about the novel, but Fleming\'s brother Peter, whose books they managed, persuaded the company to publish it.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELycett1996226_77-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELycett1996226-77">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> On 13 April 1953 <i>Casino Royale</i> was released in the UK in hardcover, priced at <a href="/wiki/%C2%A3sd" title="\xc2\xa3sd">10s 6d</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELycett1996244_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELycett1996244-78">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> with a cover designed by Fleming.<sup id="cite_ref-Guardian_covers_(2008)_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Guardian_covers_(2008)-79">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> It was a success and three print runs were needed to cope with the demand.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELycett1996244_78-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELycett1996244-78">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Guardian_covers_(2008)_79-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Guardian_covers_(2008)-79">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELindner200914_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELindner200914-80">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The novel centres on the exploits of <a href="/wiki/James_Bond_(literary_character)" title="James Bond (literary character)">James Bond</a>, an officer in the Secret Intelligence Service, commonly known as <a href="/wiki/Secret_Intelligence_Service" title="Secret Intelligence Service">MI6</a>. Bond was also known by his code number, 007, and was a <a href="/wiki/Commander_(Royal_Navy)" title="Commander (Royal Navy)">commander</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Naval_Reserve" title="Royal Naval Reserve">Royal Naval Reserve</a>. Fleming took the name for his character from that of the American ornithologist <a href="/wiki/James_Bond_(ornithologist)" title="James Bond (ornithologist)">James Bond</a>, an expert on <a href="/wiki/Caribbean" title="Caribbean">Caribbean</a> birds and author of the definitive field guide <i><a href="/wiki/Birds_of_the_West_Indies" title="Birds of the West Indies">Birds of the West Indies</a></i>. Fleming, himself a keen <a href="/wiki/Birdwatching" title="Birdwatching">birdwatcher</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Bond_obit_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bond_obit-81">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> had a copy of Bond\'s guide, and later told the ornithologist\'s wife, "that this brief, unromantic, Anglo-Saxon and yet very masculine name was just what I needed, and so a second James Bond was born".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGriswold200646_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGriswold200646-82">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> In a 1962 interview in <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_Yorker" title="The New Yorker">The New Yorker</a></i>, he further explained: "When I wrote the first one in 1953, I wanted Bond to be an extremely dull, uninteresting man to whom things happened; I wanted him to be a blunt instrument&#160;... when I was casting around for a name for my protagonist I thought by God, [James Bond] is the dullest name I ever heard."<sup id="cite_ref-Hellman_(1962)_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hellman_(1962)-83">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Fleming007impression.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/c/c5/Fleming007impression.jpg/170px-Fleming007impression.jpg" width="170" height="268" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/c/c5/Fleming007impression.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="254" data-file-height="400" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Fleming007impression.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Fleming\'s sketch showing his concept of the <a href="/wiki/James_Bond_(literary_character)" title="James Bond (literary character)">James Bond</a> character.</div></div></div>\n<p>Fleming based his creation on individuals he met during his time in the Naval Intelligence Division, and admitted that Bond "was a compound of all the secret agents and commando types I met during the war".<sup id="cite_ref-Macintyre_(2008)_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Macintyre_(2008)-84">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> Among those types were his brother Peter, whom he worshipped,<sup id="cite_ref-Macintyre_(2008)_84-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Macintyre_(2008)-84">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> and who had been involved in behind-the-lines operations in Norway and Greece during the war.<sup id="cite_ref-PF_Obit_(1971)_9-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PF_Obit_(1971)-9">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> Fleming envisaged that Bond would resemble the composer, singer and actor <a href="/wiki/Hoagy_Carmichael" title="Hoagy Carmichael">Hoagy Carmichael</a>; others, such as author and historian <a href="/wiki/Ben_Macintyre" title="Ben Macintyre">Ben Macintyre</a>, identify aspects of Fleming\'s own looks in his description of Bond.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacintyre200851_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacintyre200851-85">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAmis196635_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAmis196635-86">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> General references in the novels describe Bond as having "dark, rather cruel good looks".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenson198862_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenson198862-87">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Fleming also modelled aspects of Bond on <a href="/wiki/Conrad_O%27Brien-ffrench" title="Conrad O&#39;Brien-ffrench">Conrad O\'Brien-ffrench</a>, a spy whom Fleming had met while skiing in Kitzb\xc3\xbchel in the 1930s, <a href="/wiki/Patrick_Dalzel-Job" title="Patrick Dalzel-Job">Patrick Dalzel-Job</a>, who served with distinction in 30AU during the war, and <a href="/wiki/Wilfred_Dunderdale" title="Wilfred Dunderdale">Bill "Biffy" Dunderdale</a>, station head of MI6 in Paris, who wore cufflinks and handmade suits and was chauffeured around Paris in a <a href="/wiki/Rolls-Royce_Limited" title="Rolls-Royce Limited">Rolls-Royce</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Macintyre_(2008)_84-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Macintyre_(2008)-84">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacintyre200868\xe2\x80\x9369_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacintyre200868\xe2\x80\x9369-88">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Sir_Fitzroy_Maclean,_1st_Baronet" title="Sir Fitzroy Maclean, 1st Baronet">Sir Fitzroy Maclean</a> was another possible model for Bond, based on his wartime work behind enemy lines in the <a href="/wiki/Balkans" title="Balkans">Balkans</a>, as was the MI6 <a href="/wiki/Double_agent" title="Double agent">double agent</a> <a href="/wiki/Du%C5%A1ko_Popov" title="Du\xc5\xa1ko Popov">Du\xc5\xa1ko Popov</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChancellor200554_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChancellor200554-89">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> Fleming also endowed Bond with many of his own traits, including the same golf handicap, his taste for scrambled eggs, his love of gambling, and use of the same brand of toiletries.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacintyre200850_46-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacintyre200850-46">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Cook_(2004)_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cook_(2004)-90">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>After the publication of <i>Casino Royale</i>, Fleming used his annual holiday at his house in Jamaica to write another Bond story.<sup id="cite_ref-Lycett_(DNB)_1-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lycett_(DNB)-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> Twelve Bond novels and two short-story collections were published between 1953 and 1966, the last two (<i><a href="/wiki/The_Man_with_the_Golden_Gun_(novel)" title="The Man with the Golden Gun (novel)">The Man with the Golden Gun</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Octopussy_and_The_Living_Daylights" title="Octopussy and The Living Daylights">Octopussy and The Living Daylights</a></i>) posthumously.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlack200575_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlack200575-91">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> Much of the background to the stories came from Fleming\'s previous work in the Naval Intelligence Division or from events he knew of from the <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacintyre200890_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacintyre200890-92">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> The plot of <a href="/wiki/From_Russia,_with_Love_(novel)" title="From Russia, with Love (novel)"><i>From Russia, with Love</i></a> uses a fictional Soviet Spektor decoding machine as a lure to trap Bond; the Spektor had its roots in the wartime German Enigma machine.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChancellor200597_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChancellor200597-93">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> The novel\'s plot device of spies on the <a href="/wiki/Orient_Express" title="Orient Express">Orient Express</a> was based on the story of Eugene Karp, a US naval attach\xc3\xa9 and intelligence agent based in Budapest who took the Orient Express from Budapest to Paris in February 1950, carrying papers about blown US spy networks in the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Bloc" title="Eastern Bloc">Eastern Bloc</a>. Soviet assassins already on the train drugged the conductor, and Karp\'s body was found shortly afterwards in a railway tunnel south of <a href="/wiki/Salzburg" title="Salzburg">Salzburg</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChancellor200596_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChancellor200596-94">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:198px;"><a href="/wiki/File:HoagyCarmichael.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/e9/HoagyCarmichael.jpg" width="196" height="263" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="196" data-file-height="263" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:HoagyCarmichael.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Hoagy_Carmichael" title="Hoagy Carmichael">Hoagy Carmichael</a>, whose looks Fleming described for Bond</div></div></div>\n<p>Many of the names used in the Bond works came from people Fleming knew: <a href="/wiki/Francisco_Scaramanga" title="Francisco Scaramanga">Scaramanga</a>, the principal villain in <i>The Man with the Golden Gun</i>, was named after a fellow Eton schoolboy with whom Fleming fought;<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacintyre200890_92-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacintyre200890-92">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Auric_Goldfinger" title="Auric Goldfinger">Goldfinger</a>, from the eponymous novel, was named after British architect <a href="/wiki/Ern%C5%91_Goldfinger" title="Ern\xc5\x91 Goldfinger">Ern\xc5\x91 Goldfinger</a>, whose work Fleming abhorred;<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacintyre200890_92-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacintyre200890-92">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> Sir <a href="/wiki/Hugo_Drax" title="Hugo Drax">Hugo Drax</a>, the antagonist of <i>Moonraker</i>, was named after Fleming\'s acquaintance <a href="/wiki/Reginald_Plunkett" class="mw-redirect" title="Reginald Plunkett">Admiral Sir Reginald Aylmer Ranfurly Plunkett-Ernle-Erle-Drax</a>;<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacintyre200888_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacintyre200888-95">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> Drax\'s assistant, Krebs, bears the same name as Hitler\'s <a href="/wiki/Hans_Krebs_(Wehrmacht_general)" title="Hans Krebs (Wehrmacht general)">last Chief of Staff</a>;<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlack200520_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlack200520-96">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> and one of the homosexual villains from <a href="/wiki/Diamonds_Are_Forever_(novel)" title="Diamonds Are Forever (novel)"><i>Diamonds Are Forever</i></a>, "Boofy" Kidd, was named after one of Fleming\'s close friends\xe2\x80\x94and a relative of his wife\xe2\x80\x94<a href="/wiki/Arthur_Gore,_8th_Earl_of_Arran" title="Arthur Gore, 8th Earl of Arran">Arthur Gore, 8th Earl of Arran</a>, known as Boofy to his friends.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacintyre200890_92-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacintyre200890-92">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Fleming\'s first work of non-fiction, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Diamond_Smugglers" title="The Diamond Smugglers">The Diamond Smugglers</a></i>, was published in 1957 and was partly based on background research for his fourth Bond novel, <i>Diamonds Are Forever</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenson198816\xe2\x80\x9317_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenson198816\xe2\x80\x9317-97">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> Much of the material had appeared in <i>The Sunday Times</i> and was based on Fleming\'s interviews with John Collard, a member of the International Diamond Security Organisation who had previously worked in <a href="/wiki/MI5" title="MI5">MI5</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenson198816_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenson198816-98">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> The book received mixed reviews in the UK and US.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenson198817_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenson198817-99">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>For the first five books (<i>Casino Royale</i>, <a href="/wiki/Live_and_Let_Die_(novel)" title="Live and Let Die (novel)"><i>Live and Let Die</i></a>, <i>Moonraker</i>, <i>Diamonds Are Forever</i> and <i>From Russia, with Love</i>) Fleming received broadly positive reviews.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacintyre2008196\xe2\x80\x93197_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacintyre2008196\xe2\x80\x93197-100">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> That began to change in March 1958 when <a href="/wiki/Bernard_Bergonzi" title="Bernard Bergonzi">Bernard Bergonzi</a>, in the journal <i>Twentieth Century</i>, attacked Fleming\'s work as containing "a strongly marked streak of voyeurism and sado-masochism"<sup id="cite_ref-Bergonzi_(1958)_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bergonzi_(1958)-101">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> and wrote that the books showed "the total lack of any ethical frame of reference".<sup id="cite_ref-Bergonzi_(1958)_101-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bergonzi_(1958)-101">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> The article compared Fleming unfavourably with <a href="/wiki/John_Buchan,_1st_Baron_Tweedsmuir" class="mw-redirect" title="John Buchan, 1st Baron Tweedsmuir">John Buchan</a> and Raymond Chandler on both moral and literary criteria.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELindner200919_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELindner200919-102">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> A month later, <i><a href="/wiki/Dr._No_(novel)" title="Dr. No (novel)">Dr. No</a></i> was published, and Fleming received harsh criticism from reviewers who, in the words of Ben Macintyre, "rounded on Fleming, almost as a pack".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacintyre2008197_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacintyre2008197-103">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> The most strongly worded of the critiques came from <a href="/wiki/Paul_Johnson_(writer)" title="Paul Johnson (writer)">Paul Johnson</a> of the <i><a href="/wiki/New_Statesman" title="New Statesman">New Statesman</a></i>, who, in his review "Sex, Snobbery and Sadism", called the novel "without doubt, the nastiest book I have ever read".<sup id="cite_ref-Johnson_(1958)_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Johnson_(1958)-104">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> Johnson went on to say that "by the time I was a third of the way through, I had to suppress a strong impulse to throw the thing away".<sup id="cite_ref-Johnson_(1958)_104-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Johnson_(1958)-104">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> Johnson recognised that in Bond there "was a social phenomenon of some importance",<sup id="cite_ref-Johnson_(1958)_104-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Johnson_(1958)-104">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> but this was seen as a negative element, as the phenomenon concerned "three basic ingredients in <i>Dr No</i>, all unhealthy, all thoroughly English: the sadism of a schoolboy bully, the mechanical, two-dimensional sex-longings of a frustrated adolescent, and the crude, snob-cravings of a suburban adult."<sup id="cite_ref-Johnson_(1958)_104-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Johnson_(1958)-104">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> Johnson saw no positives in <i>Dr. No</i>, and said, "Mr Fleming has no literary skill, the construction of the book is chaotic, and entire incidents and situations are inserted, and then forgotten, in a haphazard manner."<sup id="cite_ref-Johnson_(1958)_104-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Johnson_(1958)-104">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Lycett notes that Fleming "went into a personal and creative decline" after marital problems and the attacks on his work.<sup id="cite_ref-Lycett_(DNB)_1-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lycett_(DNB)-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Goldfinger_(novel)" title="Goldfinger (novel)"><i>Goldfinger</i></a> had been written before the publication of <i>Dr. No</i>; the next book Fleming produced after the criticism was <a href="/wiki/For_Your_Eyes_Only_(short_story_collection)" title="For Your Eyes Only (short story collection)"><i>For Your Eyes Only</i></a>, a collection of short stories derived from outlines written for a television series that did not come to fruition.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenson198818_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenson198818-105">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> Lycett noted that, as Fleming was writing the television scripts and the short stories, "Ian\'s mood of weariness and self-doubt was beginning to affect his writing", which can be seen in Bond\'s thoughts.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELycett1996369_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELycett1996369-106">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1960s">1960s</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ian_Fleming&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: 1960s">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>In 1960 Fleming was commissioned by the <a href="/wiki/Kuwait_Oil_Company" title="Kuwait Oil Company">Kuwait Oil Company</a> to write a book on the country and its oil industry. The Kuwaiti Government disapproved of the typescript, <i>State of Excitement: Impressions of Kuwait</i>, and it was never published. According to Fleming: "The Oil Company expressed approval of the book but felt it their duty to submit the typescript to members of the Kuwait Government for their approval. The <a href="/wiki/Sheikh" title="Sheikh">Sheikhs</a> concerned found unpalatable certain mild comments and criticisms and particularly the passages referring to the adventurous past of the country which now wishes to be \'civilised\' in every respect and forget its romantic origins."<sup id="cite_ref-Lilly_Library_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lilly_Library-107">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Fleming followed the disappointment of <i>For Your Eyes Only</i> with <a href="/wiki/Thunderball_(novel)" title="Thunderball (novel)"><i>Thunderball</i></a>, the <a href="/wiki/Novelization" title="Novelization">novelization</a> of a film script on which he had worked with others. The work had started in 1958 when Fleming\'s friend Ivar Bryce introduced him to a young Irish writer and director, <a href="/wiki/Kevin_McClory" title="Kevin McClory">Kevin McClory</a>, and the three, together with Fleming and Bryce\'s friend Ernest Cuneo, worked on a script.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenson198817_99-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenson198817-99">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> In October McClory introduced experienced screenwriter <a href="/wiki/Jack_Whittingham" title="Jack Whittingham">Jack Whittingham</a> to the newly formed team,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPearson1967374_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPearson1967374-108">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> and by December 1959 McClory and Whittingham sent Fleming a script.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenson1988231_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenson1988231-109">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> Fleming had been having second thoughts on McClory\'s involvement and, in January 1960, explained his intention of delivering the screenplay to <a href="/wiki/Music_Corporation_of_America" class="mw-redirect" title="Music Corporation of America">MCA</a>, with a recommendation from him and Bryce that McClory act as producer.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPearson1967381_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPearson1967381-110">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> He additionally told McClory that if MCA rejected the film because of McClory\'s involvement, then McClory should either sell himself to MCA, back out of the deal, or file a suit in court.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPearson1967381_110-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPearson1967381-110">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Working at Goldeneye between January and March 1960, Fleming wrote the novel <i>Thunderball</i>, based on the screenplay written by himself, Whittingham and McClory.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenson198820_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenson198820-111">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> In March 1961 McClory read an advance copy, and he and Whittingham immediately petitioned the <a href="/wiki/High_Court_of_Justice" title="High Court of Justice">High Court</a> in London for an injunction to stop publication.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenson198821_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenson198821-112">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> After <a href="/wiki/Thunderball_(novel)#Controversy" title="Thunderball (novel)">two court actions</a>, the second in November 1961,<sup id="cite_ref-Sellers_(2007)_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sellers_(2007)-113">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> Fleming offered McClory a deal, settling out of court. McClory gained the literary and film rights for the screenplay, while Fleming was given the rights to the novel, provided it was acknowledged as "based on a screen treatment by Kevin McClory, Jack Whittingham and the Author".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELycett1996432_114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELycett1996432-114">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Fleming\'s books had always sold well, but in 1961 sales increased dramatically. On 17 March 1961, four years after its publication and three years after the heavy criticism of <i>Dr. No</i>, an article in <i><a href="/wiki/Life_(magazine)" title="Life (magazine)">Life</a></i> listed <i>From Russia, with Love</i> as one of <a href="/wiki/President_of_the_United_States" title="President of the United States">US President</a> <a href="/wiki/John_F._Kennedy" title="John F. Kennedy">John F. Kennedy</a>\'s ten favourite books.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> Kennedy and Fleming had previously met in Washington.<sup id="cite_ref-Hellman_(1962)_83-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hellman_(1962)-83">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> This accolade and the associated publicity led to a surge in sales that made Fleming the biggest-selling crime writer in the US.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELycett1996383_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELycett1996383-116">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFlemingHigson2006vi_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFlemingHigson2006vi-117">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> Fleming considered <i>From Russia, with Love</i> to be his best novel; he said "the great thing is that each one of the books seems to have been a favourite with one or other section of the public and none has yet been completely damned."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChancellor200597_93-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChancellor200597-93">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In April 1961, shortly before the second court case on <i>Thunderball</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-Lycett_(DNB)_1-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lycett_(DNB)-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> Fleming had a heart attack during a regular weekly meeting at <i>The Sunday Times</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELycett1996384_67-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELycett1996384-67">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> While he was convalescing, one of his friends, Duff Dunbar, gave him a copy of <a href="/wiki/Beatrix_Potter" title="Beatrix Potter">Beatrix Potter</a>\'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Tale_of_Squirrel_Nutkin" title="The Tale of Squirrel Nutkin">The Tale of Squirrel Nutkin</a></i> and suggested that he take the time to write up the bedtime story that Fleming used to tell to his son Caspar each evening.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELycett1996384_67-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELycett1996384-67">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> Fleming attacked the project with gusto and wrote to his publisher, Michael Howard of Jonathan Cape, joking that "There is not a moment, even on the edge of the tomb, when I am not slaving for you";<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacintyre2008194_118-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacintyre2008194-118">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> the result was Fleming\'s only children\'s novel, <i><a href="/wiki/Chitty-Chitty-Bang-Bang" title="Chitty-Chitty-Bang-Bang">Chitty-Chitty-Bang-Bang</a></i>, which was published in October 1964, two months after his death.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenson198827_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenson198827-119">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In June 1961 Fleming sold a six-month option on the film rights to his published and future James Bond novels and short stories to <a href="/wiki/Harry_Saltzman" title="Harry Saltzman">Harry Saltzman</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenson198821_112-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenson198821-112">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> Saltzman formed the production vehicle <a href="/wiki/Eon_Productions" title="Eon Productions">Eon Productions</a> along with  <a href="/wiki/Albert_R._Broccoli" title="Albert R. Broccoli">Albert R. "Cubby" Broccoli</a>, and after an extensive search, they hired <a href="/wiki/Sean_Connery" title="Sean Connery">Sean Connery</a> on a five-film deal, beginning with <i><a href="/wiki/Dr._No_(film)" title="Dr. No (film)">Dr. No</a></i> (1962).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenson198822_120-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenson198822-120">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-documentary_121-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-documentary-121">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> Connery\'s depiction of Bond affected the literary character; in <i><a href="/wiki/You_Only_Live_Twice_(novel)" title="You Only Live Twice (novel)">You Only Live Twice</a></i>, the first book written after <i>Dr. No</i> was released, Fleming gave Bond a sense of humour that was not present in the previous stories.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacintyre2008205_122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacintyre2008205-122">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Fleming\'s second non-fiction book was published in November 1963: <i><a href="/wiki/Thrilling_Cities" title="Thrilling Cities">Thrilling Cities</a></i>,<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup> a reprint of a series of <i>Sunday Times</i> articles based on Fleming\'s impressions of world cities<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPearson1967375_124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPearson1967375-124">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> in trips taken during 1959 and
