1960.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFleming19637_125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFleming19637-125">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup> Approached in 1964 by producer <a href="/wiki/Norman_Felton" title="Norman Felton">Norman Felton</a> to write a spy series for television, Fleming provided several ideas, including the names of characters <a href="/wiki/Napoleon_Solo" title="Napoleon Solo">Napoleon Solo</a> and <a href="/wiki/The_Girl_from_U.N.C.L.E." title="The Girl from U.N.C.L.E.">April Dancer</a>, for the series <i><a href="/wiki/The_Man_from_U.N.C.L.E." title="The Man from U.N.C.L.E.">The Man from U.N.C.L.E.</a></i><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenson198826_126-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenson198826-126">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup> However, Fleming withdrew from the project following a request from Eon Productions, who were keen to avoid any legal problems that might occur if the project overlapped with the Bond films.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBritton200436_127-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBritton200436-127">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In January 1964 Fleming went to Goldeneye for what proved to be his last holiday and wrote the first draft of <i>The Man with the Golden Gun</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDelFattore1989108_128-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDelFattore1989108-128">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup> He was dissatisfied with it and wrote to <a href="/wiki/William_Plomer" title="William Plomer">William Plomer</a>, the copy editor of his novels, asking for it to be rewritten.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenson198830_129-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenson198830-129">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> Fleming became increasingly unhappy with the book and considered rewriting it, but was dissuaded by Plomer, who considered it viable for publication.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELycett1996438_130-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELycett1996438-130">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Death">Death</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ian_Fleming&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Death">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ian_fleming_grave.png" class="image"><img alt="An obelisk marking the site of the Fleming family grave" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Ian_fleming_grave.png/170px-Ian_fleming_grave.png" width="170" height="419" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Ian_fleming_grave.png/255px-Ian_fleming_grave.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/c3/Ian_fleming_grave.png 2x" data-file-width="319" data-file-height="787" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ian_fleming_grave.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ian Fleming\'s grave and memorial at <a href="/wiki/Sevenhampton,_Wiltshire" title="Sevenhampton, Wiltshire">Sevenhampton</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Fleming was a heavy <a href="/wiki/Tobacco_smoking" title="Tobacco smoking">smoker</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alcoholic_beverage" class="mw-redirect" title="Alcoholic beverage">drinker</a> throughout his adult life, and suffered from <a href="/wiki/Heart_disease" class="mw-redirect" title="Heart disease">heart disease</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">&#91;d&#93;</a></sup> In 1961, aged 53, he suffered a <a href="/wiki/Myocardial_infarction" title="Myocardial infarction">heart attack</a> and struggled to <a href="/wiki/Recuperation_(recovery)" class="mw-redirect" title="Recuperation (recovery)">recuperate</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELycett1996442_134-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELycett1996442-134">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup> On 11 August 1964, while staying at a hotel in <a href="/wiki/Canterbury" title="Canterbury">Canterbury</a>, Fleming went to the <a href="/wiki/Royal_St_George%27s_Golf_Club" title="Royal St George&#39;s Golf Club">Royal St George\'s Golf Club</a> for lunch and later dined at his hotel with friends. The day had been tiring for him, and he collapsed with another heart attack shortly after the meal.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELycett1996442_134-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELycett1996442-134">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup> Fleming died at age 56 in the early morning of 12 August 1964\xe2\x80\x94his son Caspar\'s twelfth birthday.<sup id="cite_ref-Times_obit_(1965)_135-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Times_obit_(1965)-135">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> His last recorded words were an apology to the ambulance drivers for having inconvenienced them,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDelFattore1989110_137-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDelFattore1989110-137">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup> saying "I am sorry to trouble you chaps. I don\'t know how you get along so fast with the traffic on the roads these days."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELycett1996443_138-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELycett1996443-138">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup> Fleming was buried in the churchyard of <a href="/wiki/Sevenhampton,_Wiltshire" title="Sevenhampton, Wiltshire">Sevenhampton</a>, near <a href="/wiki/Swindon" title="Swindon">Swindon</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWinn2012247_139-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWinn2012247-139">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Fleming\'s last two books, <i>The Man with the Golden Gun</i> and <i>Octopussy and The Living Daylights</i>, were published posthumously.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlack200575_91-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlack200575-91">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> <i>The Man with the Golden Gun</i> was published eight months after Fleming\'s death and had not been through the full editing process by Fleming.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenson1988141_140-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenson1988141-140">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup> As a result, the novel was thought by publishing company Jonathan Cape to be thin and "feeble".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELycett1996445_141-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELycett1996445-141">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup> The publishers had passed the manuscript to <a href="/wiki/Kingsley_Amis" title="Kingsley Amis">Kingsley Amis</a> to read on holiday, but did not use his suggestions.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELycett1996445_141-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELycett1996445-141">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup> Fleming\'s biographer Henry Chandler observes that the novel "received polite and rather sad reviews, recognising that the book had effectively been left half-finished, and as such did not represent Fleming at the top of his game".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChancellor2005233_142-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChancellor2005233-142">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> The final Bond book, containing two short stories, <i>Octopussy and The Living Daylights</i>, was published in Britain on 23 June 1966.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenson198831_143-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenson198831-143">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In October 1975, Fleming\'s son Caspar, aged 23, committed suicide by <a href="/wiki/Drug_overdose" title="Drug overdose">drug overdose</a><sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup> and was buried with his father.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWinn2012247_139-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWinn2012247-139">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> Fleming\'s widow, Ann, died in 1981 and was buried with her husband and their son.<sup id="cite_ref-Lycett_(Ann_DNB)_26-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lycett_(Ann_DNB)-26">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Writing">Writing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ian_Fleming&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Writing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>The author <a href="/wiki/Raymond_Benson" title="Raymond Benson">Raymond Benson</a>, who later wrote a series of Bond novels, noted that Fleming\'s books fall into two distinct periods along stylistic lines. Those books written between 1953 and 1960 tend to concentrate on "mood, character development, and plot advancement",<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenson198885_145-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenson198885-145">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup> while those released between 1961 and 1966 incorporate more detail and imagery. Benson argues that Fleming had become "a master storyteller" by the time he wrote <i>Thunderball</i> in 1961.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenson198831_143-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenson198831-143">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Jeremy_Black_(historian)" title="Jeremy Black (historian)">Jeremy Black</a> divides the series based on the villains Fleming created, a division supported by fellow academic Christoph Linder.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELindner200981_146-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELindner200981-146">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup> Thus the early books from <i>Casino Royale</i> to <i>For Your Eyes Only</i> are classed as "Cold War stories", with <a href="/wiki/SMERSH" title="SMERSH">SMERSH</a> as the antagonists,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlack200549_147-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlack200549-147">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup> followed by <a href="/wiki/Ernst_Stavro_Blofeld" title="Ernst Stavro Blofeld">Blofeld</a> and <a href="/wiki/SPECTRE" title="SPECTRE">SPECTRE</a> as Bond\'s opponents in the three novels <i>Thunderball</i>, <i>On Her Majesty\'s Secret Service</i> and <i>You Only Live Twice</i>, after the thawing of East\xe2\x80\x93West relations.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlack2005v_148-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlack2005v-148">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">&#91;e&#93;</a></sup> Black and Linder both classify the remaining books\xe2\x80\x94<i>The Man with the Golden Gun</i>, <i>Octopussy and The Living Daylights</i> and <i>The Spy Who Loved Me</i>\xe2\x80\x94as "the later Fleming stories".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlack200571_151-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlack200571-151">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Style_and_technique">Style and technique</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ian_Fleming&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Style and technique">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Fleming said of his work, "while thrillers may not be Literature with a capital L, it is possible to write what I can best describe as \'thrillers designed to be read as literature<span style="padding-right:.2em;">\'</span>".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFaulksFleming2009320_152-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFaulksFleming2009320-152">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup> He named <a href="/wiki/Raymond_Chandler" title="Raymond Chandler">Raymond Chandler</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dashiell_Hammett" title="Dashiell Hammett">Dashiell Hammett</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eric_Ambler" title="Eric Ambler">Eric Ambler</a> and <a href="/wiki/Graham_Greene" title="Graham Greene">Graham Greene</a> as influences.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBennettWoollacott200313_153-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBennettWoollacott200313-153">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup> William Cook in the <i><a href="/wiki/New_Statesman" title="New Statesman">New Statesman</a></i> considered James Bond to be "the culmination of an important but much-maligned tradition in English literature. As a boy, Fleming devoured the <a href="/wiki/Bulldog_Drummond" title="Bulldog Drummond">Bulldog Drummond</a> tales of Lieutenant Colonel <a href="/wiki/H._C._McNeile" title="H. C. McNeile">H. C. McNeile</a> (aka "Sapper") and the <a href="/wiki/Richard_Hannay" title="Richard Hannay">Richard Hannay</a> stories of John Buchan. His genius was to repackage these antiquated adventures to fit the fashion of postwar Britain&#160;... In Bond, he created a Bulldog Drummond for the jet age."<sup id="cite_ref-Cook_(2004)_90-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cook_(2004)-90">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Umberto_Eco" title="Umberto Eco">Umberto Eco</a> considered <a href="/wiki/Mickey_Spillane" title="Mickey Spillane">Mickey Spillane</a> to have been another major influence.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEco200334_154-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEco200334-154">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In May 1963 Fleming wrote a piece for <i>Books and Bookmen</i> magazine in which he described his approach to writing Bond books: "I write for about three hours in the morning&#160;... and I do another hour\'s work between six and seven in the evening. I never correct anything and I never go back to see what I have written&#160;... By following my formula, you write 2,000 words a day."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFaulksFleming2009320_152-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFaulksFleming2009320-152">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup> Benson identified what he described as the "Fleming Sweep", the use of "hooks" at the end of chapters to heighten tension and pull the reader into the next.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenson198885_145-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenson198885-145">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup> The hooks combine with what <a href="/wiki/Anthony_Burgess" title="Anthony Burgess">Anthony Burgess</a> calls "a heightened journalistic style"<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurgess198474_155-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurgess198474-155">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup> to produce "a speed of narrative, which hustles the reader past each danger point of mockery".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFaulksFleming2009318_156-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFaulksFleming2009318-156">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Umberto Eco analysed Fleming\'s works from a <a href="/wiki/Structuralism" title="Structuralism">Structuralist</a> point of view,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELindner20093_157-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELindner20093-157">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup> and identified a series of oppositions within the storylines that provide structure and narrative, including:\n</p>\n<div>\n\n&#32;\n<table class="multicol" role="presentation" style="border-collapse: collapse; padding: 0; border: 0; background:transparent; width:100%;"><tbody><tr>\n<td style="width: 50%;text-align: left; vertical-align: top;">\n<ul><li>Bond\xe2\x80\x94M</li>\n<li>Bond\xe2\x80\x94Villain</li>\n<li>Villain\xe2\x80\x94Woman</li>\n<li>Woman\xe2\x80\x94Bond</li>\n<li>Free World\xe2\x80\x94Soviet Union</li>\n<li>Great Britain\xe2\x80\x94Non-Anglo-Saxon Countries</li>\n<li>Duty\xe2\x80\x94Sacrifice</li></ul>\n<p>&#32;\n</p>\n</td>\n<td style="text-align: left; vertical-align: top;">\n<ul><li>Cupidity\xe2\x80\x94Ideals</li>\n<li>Love\xe2\x80\x94Death</li>\n<li>Chance\xe2\x80\x94Planning</li>\n<li>Luxury\xe2\x80\x94Discomfort</li>\n<li>Excess\xe2\x80\x94Moderation</li>\n<li>Perversion\xe2\x80\x94Innocence</li>\n<li>Loyalty\xe2\x80\x94Dishonour<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEco200336_158-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEco200336-158">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>\n<p>&#32;\n</p>\n</td></tr></tbody></table></div>\n<p>Eco also noted that the Bond villains tend to come from Central Europe or from Slavic or Mediterranean countries and have a mixed heritage and "complex and obscure origins".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEco200340_159-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEco200340-159">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup> Eco found that the villains were generally asexual or homosexual, inventive, organisationally astute, and wealthy.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEco200340_159-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEco200340-159">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup> Black observed the same point: "Fleming did not use class enemies for his villains instead relying on physical distortion or ethnic identity&#160;... Furthermore, in Britain foreign villains used foreign servants and employees&#160;... This racism reflected not only a pronounced theme of interwar adventure writing, such as the novels of Buchan, but also wider literary culture."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlack200519_160-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlack200519-160">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup> Writer <a href="/wiki/Louise_Welsh" title="Louise Welsh">Louise Welsh</a> found that the novel <i>Live and Let Die</i> "taps into the paranoia that some sectors of white society were feeling" as the <a href="/wiki/Civil_rights_movement" title="Civil rights movement">civil rights movements</a> challenged prejudice and inequality.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFlemingWelsh2006v_161-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFlemingWelsh2006v-161">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Fleming used well-known brand names and everyday details to support a sense of realism.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFaulksFleming2009320_152-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFaulksFleming2009320-152">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup> Kingsley Amis called this "the Fleming effect",<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAmis1966112_162-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAmis1966112-162">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup> describing it as "the imaginative use of information, whereby the pervading fantastic nature of Bond\'s world&#160;... [is] bolted down to some sort of reality, or at least counter-balanced."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAmis1966111\xe2\x80\x93112_163-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAmis1966111\xe2\x80\x93112-163">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Major_themes">Major themes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ian_Fleming&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Major themes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<h4><span id="Britain.27s_position_in_the_world"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Britain\'s_position_in_the_world">Britain\'s position in the world</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ian_Fleming&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Britain&#039;s position in the world">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>The Bond books were written in post-war Britain, when the country was still an imperial power.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlack20053_164-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlack20053-164">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup> As the series progressed, the <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British Empire</a> was in decline; journalist William Cook observed that "Bond pandered to Britain\'s inflated and increasingly insecure self-image, flattering us with the fantasy that Britannia could still punch above her weight."<sup id="cite_ref-Cook_(2004)_90-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cook_(2004)-90">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> This decline of British power was referred to in several of the novels; in <i>From Russia, with Love</i>, it manifested itself in Bond\'s conversations with Darko Kerim, when Bond admits that in England, "we don\'t show teeth any more\xe2\x80\x94only gums."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFlemingHigson2006227_165-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFlemingHigson2006227-165">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacintyre2008113_166-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacintyre2008113-166">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup> The theme is strongest in one of the later books of the series, the 1964 novel <i>You Only Live Twice</i>, in conversations between Bond and the head of Japan\'s secret intelligence service, <a href="/wiki/Tiger_Tanaka" class="mw-redirect" title="Tiger Tanaka">Tiger Tanaka</a>. Fleming was acutely aware of the loss of British prestige in the 1950s and early 60s, particularly during the <a href="/wiki/Indonesia%E2%80%93Malaysia_confrontation" title="Indonesia\xe2\x80\x93Malaysia confrontation">Indonesia\xe2\x80\x93Malaysia confrontation</a>, when he had Tanaka accuse Britain of throwing away the empire "with both hands".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacintyre2008113_166-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacintyre2008113-166">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChancellor2005200\xe2\x80\x93201_167-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChancellor2005200\xe2\x80\x93201-167">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlack200562_168-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlack200562-168">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Black points to the defections of <a href="/wiki/Cambridge_Five" title="Cambridge Five">four members of MI6</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a> as having a major impact on how Britain was viewed in US intelligence circles.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlack200561_169-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlack200561-169">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup> The last of the defections was that of <a href="/wiki/Kim_Philby" title="Kim Philby">Kim Philby</a> in January 1963,<sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup> while Fleming was still writing the first draft of <i>You Only Live Twice</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenson198824_171-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenson198824-171">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup> The briefing between Bond and M is the first time in the twelve books that Fleming acknowledges the defections.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChancellor2005200_172-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChancellor2005200-172">&#91;167&#93;</a></sup> Black contends that the conversation between M and Bond allows Fleming to discuss the decline of Britain, with the defections and the <a href="/wiki/Profumo_Affair" class="mw-redirect" title="Profumo Affair">Profumo Affair</a> of 1963 as a backdrop.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlack200562_168-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlack200562-168">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup> Two of the defections had taken place shortly before Fleming wrote <i>Casino Royale</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-173" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-173">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup> and the book can be seen as the writer\'s "attempt to reflect the disturbing moral ambiguity of a post-war world that could produce traitors like <a href="/wiki/Guy_Burgess" title="Guy Burgess">Burgess</a> and <a href="/wiki/Donald_Maclean_(spy)" title="Donald Maclean (spy)">Maclean</a>", according to Lycett.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELycett1996221_174-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELycett1996221-174">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>By the end of the series, in the 1965 novel, <i>The Man with the Golden Gun</i>, Black notes that an independent inquiry was undertaken by the Jamaican judiciary, while the CIA and MI6 were recorded as acting "under the closest liaison and direction of the Jamaican CID": this was the new world of a non-colonial, independent Jamaica, further underlining the decline of the British Empire.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlack200578_175-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlack200578-175">&#91;170&#93;</a></sup> The decline was also reflected in Bond\'s use of US equipment and personnel in several novels.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlack200553\xe2\x80\x9354_176-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlack200553\xe2\x80\x9354-176">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup> Uncertain and shifting geopolitics led Fleming to replace the Russian organisation SMERSH with the international terrorist group SPECTRE in <i>Thunderball</i>, permitting "evil unconstrained by ideology".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlack200550_177-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlack200550-177">&#91;172&#93;</a></sup> Black argues that SPECTRE provides a measure of continuity to the remaining stories in the series.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlack200549_147-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlack200549-147">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Effects_of_the_war">Effects of the war</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ian_Fleming&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Effects of the war">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>A theme throughout the series was the effect of the Second World War.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlack20057_178-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlack20057-178">&#91;173&#93;</a></sup> <i>The Times</i> journalist Ben Macintyre considers that Bond was "the ideal antidote to Britain\'s postwar austerity, rationing and the looming premonition of lost power",<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacintyre200885\xe2\x80\x9386_179-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacintyre200885\xe2\x80\x9386-179">&#91;174&#93;</a></sup> at a time when coal and many items of food were still rationed.<sup id="cite_ref-Cook_(2004)_90-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cook_(2004)-90">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> Fleming often used the war as a signal to establish good or evil in characters:<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlack200520_96-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlack200520-96">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlack200559_180-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlack200559-180">&#91;175&#93;</a></sup> in <a href="/wiki/For_Your_Eyes_Only_(short_story_collection)#&quot;For_Your_Eyes_Only&quot;" title="For Your Eyes Only (short story collection)"><i>For Your Eyes Only</i></a>, the villain, Hammerstein, is a former <a href="/wiki/Gestapo" title="Gestapo">Gestapo</a> officer, while the sympathetic <a href="/wiki/Royal_Canadian_Mounted_Police" title="Royal Canadian Mounted Police">Royal Canadian Mounted Police</a> officer, Colonel Johns, served with the British under <a href="/wiki/Bernard_Montgomery,_1st_Viscount_Montgomery_of_Alamein" class="mw-redirect" title="Bernard Montgomery, 1st Viscount Montgomery of Alamein">Montgomery</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Eighth_Army_(United_Kingdom)" title="Eighth Army (United Kingdom)">Eighth Army</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlack200541_181-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlack200541-181">&#91;176&#93;</a></sup> Similarly, in <i>Moonraker</i>, Drax (Graf Hugo von der Drache) is a "megalomaniac German Nazi who masquerades as an English gentleman",<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlack200581_182-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlack200581-182">&#91;177&#93;</a></sup> and his assistant, Krebs, bears the same name as Hitler\'s last Chief of Staff.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlack200520_96-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlack200520-96">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> In this, Fleming "exploits another British cultural antipathy of the 1950s. Germans, in the wake of the Second World War, made another easy and obvious target for bad press."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlack200581_182-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlack200581-182">&#91;177&#93;</a></sup> As the series progressed, the threat of a re-emergent Germany was overtaken by concerns about the Cold War, and the novels changed their focus accordingly.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlack2005x_183-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlack2005x-183">&#91;178&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Comradeship">Comradeship</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ian_Fleming&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Comradeship">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>Periodically in the series, the topic of comradeship or friendship arises, with a male ally who works with Bond on his mission.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenson198886_184-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenson198886-184">&#91;179&#93;</a></sup> Raymond Benson believes that the relationships Bond has with his allies "add another dimension to Bond\'s character, and ultimately, to the thematic continuity of the novels".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenson198887_185-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenson198887-185">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup> In <i>Live and Let Die</i>, agents Quarrel and <a href="/wiki/Felix_Leiter" title="Felix Leiter">Leiter</a> represent the importance of male friends and allies, seen especially in  Bond\'s response to the shark attack on Leiter; Benson observes that "the loyalty Bond feels towards his friends is as strong as his commitment to his job".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenson198896_186-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenson198896-186">&#91;181&#93;</a></sup> In <i>Dr. No</i>, Quarrel is "an indispensable ally".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELindner200967_187-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELindner200967-187">&#91;182&#93;</a></sup> Benson sees no evidence of discrimination in their relationship<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenson1988112_188-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenson1988112-188">&#91;183&#93;</a></sup> and notes Bond\'s genuine remorse and sadness at Quarrel\'s death.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenson1988110_189-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenson1988110-189">&#91;184&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span id="The_.22traitor_within.22"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="The_&quot;traitor_within&quot;">The "traitor within"</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ian_Fleming&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: The &quot;traitor within&quot;">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>From the opening novel in the series, the theme of treachery was strong. Bond\'s target in <i>Casino Royale</i>, <a href="/wiki/Le_Chiffre" title="Le Chiffre">Le Chiffre</a>, was the paymaster of a French communist trade union, and the overtones of a fifth column struck a chord with the largely British readership, as Communist influence in the trade unions had been an issue in the press and parliament,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlack20055_190-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlack20055-190">&#91;185&#93;</a></sup> especially after the defections of Burgess and Maclean in 1951.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELycett1996221_174-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELycett1996221-174">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup> The "traitor within" theme continued in <i>Live and Let Die</i> and <i>Moonraker</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlack200516_191-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlack200516-191">&#91;186&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Good_versus_evil">Good versus evil</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ian_Fleming&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Good versus evil">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>Raymond Benson considered the most obvious theme of the series to be good versus evil.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenson198886_184-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenson198886-184">&#91;179&#93;</a></sup> This crystallised in <i>Goldfinger</i> with the <a href="/wiki/Saint_George" title="Saint George">Saint George</a> motif, which is stated explicitly in the book:<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenson1988231_109-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenson1988231-109">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> "Bond sighed wearily. Once more into the breach, dear friend! This time, it really was St George and the dragon. And St George had better get a move on and do something";<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFleming2006ch_18_192-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFleming2006ch_18-192">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup> Black notes that the image of St. George is an English, rather than British personification.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlack200539_193-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlack200539-193">&#91;188&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Anglo-American_relations">Anglo-American relations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ian_Fleming&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Anglo-American relations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>The Bond novels also dealt with the question of Anglo-American relations, reflecting the central role of the US in the defence of the West.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlack20056_194-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlack20056-194">&#91;189&#93;</a></sup> In the aftermath of the Second World War, tensions surfaced between a British government trying to retain its empire and the American desire for a capitalist new world order, but Fleming did not focus on this directly, instead creating "an impression of the normality of British imperial rule and action".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlack20057_178-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlack20057-178">&#91;173&#93;</a></sup> Author and journalist <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Hitchens" title="Christopher Hitchens">Christopher Hitchens</a> observed that "the central paradox of the classic Bond stories is that, although superficially devoted to the Anglo-American war against communism, they are full of contempt and resentment for America and Americans".<sup id="cite_ref-Hitchens_(2006)_195-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hitchens_(2006)-195">&#91;190&#93;</a></sup> Fleming was aware of this tension between the two countries, but did not focus on it strongly.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlack20057_178-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlack20057-178">&#91;173&#93;</a></sup> Kingsley Amis, in his exploration of Bond in <i><a href="/wiki/The_James_Bond_Dossier" title="The James Bond Dossier">The James Bond Dossier</a></i>, pointed out that "Leiter, such a nonentity as a piece of characterization&#160;... he, the American, takes orders from Bond, the Britisher, and that Bond is constantly doing better than he".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAmis196690_196-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAmis196690-196">&#91;191&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>For three of the novels, <i>Goldfinger</i>, <i>Live and Let Die</i> and <i>Dr. No</i>, it is Bond the British agent who has to sort out what turns out to be an American problem,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlack200538\xe2\x80\x9339_197-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlack200538\xe2\x80\x9339-197">&#91;192&#93;</a></sup> and Black points out that although it is American assets that are under threat in <i>Dr. No</i>, a British agent and a British warship, HMS <i>Narvik</i>, are sent with British soldiers to the island at the end of the novel to settle the matter.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlack200533_198-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlack200533-198">&#91;193&#93;</a></sup> Fleming became increasingly jaundiced about America, and his comments in the penultimate novel <i>You Only Live Twice</i> reflect this;<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacintyre2008187_199-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacintyre2008187-199">&#91;194&#93;</a></sup> Bond\'s responses to Tanaka\'s comments reflect the declining relationship between Britain and America\xe2\x80\x94in sharp contrast to the warm, co-operative relationship between Bond and Leiter in the earlier books.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlack200562_168-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlack200562-168">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">Legacy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ian_Fleming&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Legacy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ian-Fleming-bronze-bust-by-sculptor-Anthony-Smith.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Ian-Fleming-bronze-bust-by-sculptor-Anthony-Smith.jpg/170px-Ian-Fleming-bronze-bust-by-sculptor-Anthony-Smith.jpg" width="170" height="212" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Ian-Fleming-bronze-bust-by-sculptor-Anthony-Smith.jpg/255px-Ian-Fleming-bronze-bust-by-sculptor-Anthony-Smith.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Ian-Fleming-bronze-bust-by-sculptor-Anthony-Smith.jpg/340px-Ian-Fleming-bronze-bust-by-sculptor-Anthony-Smith.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1028" data-file-height="1280" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ian-Fleming-bronze-bust-by-sculptor-Anthony-Smith.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bronze <a href="/wiki/Bust_(sculpture)" title="Bust (sculpture)">bust</a> of Fleming by sculptor <a href="/wiki/Anthony_Smith_(sculptor)" title="Anthony Smith (sculptor)">Anthony Smith</a>, commissioned by the Fleming family in 2008 to commemorate the <a href="/wiki/Centenary" class="mw-redirect" title="Centenary">centenary</a> of the author\'s birth.<sup id="cite_ref-200" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-200">&#91;195&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>In the late 1950s the author <a href="/wiki/Geoffrey_Jenkins" title="Geoffrey Jenkins">Geoffrey Jenkins</a> had suggested to Fleming that he write a Bond novel set in South Africa, and sent him his own idea for a plot outline which, according to Jenkins, Fleming felt had great potential.<sup id="cite_ref-KKBB:_PFO_201-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-KKBB:_PFO-201">&#91;196&#93;</a></sup> After Fleming\'s death, Jenkins was commissioned by Bond publishers <a href="/wiki/Ian_Fleming_Publications" title="Ian Fleming Publications">Glidrose Productions</a> to write a <a href="/wiki/Continuation_novel" title="Continuation novel">continuation</a> Bond novel, <i><a href="/wiki/Per_Fine_Ounce" title="Per Fine Ounce">Per Fine Ounce</a></i>, but it was never published.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELaneSimpson2000433_202-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELaneSimpson2000433-202">&#91;197&#93;</a></sup> Starting with Kingsley Amis\'s <i><a href="/wiki/Colonel_Sun" title="Colonel Sun">Colonel Sun</a></i>, under the pseudonym "<a href="/wiki/Robert_Markham" title="Robert Markham">Robert Markham</a>" in 1968,<sup id="cite_ref-203" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-203">&#91;198&#93;</a></sup> several authors have been commissioned to write Bond novels, including <a href="/wiki/Sebastian_Faulks" title="Sebastian Faulks">Sebastian Faulks</a>, who was asked by <a href="/wiki/Ian_Fleming_Publications" title="Ian Fleming Publications">Ian Fleming Publications</a> to write a new Bond novel in observance of what would have been Fleming\'s 100th birthday in 2008.<sup id="cite_ref-204" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-204">&#91;199&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>During his lifetime Fleming sold thirty million books; double that number were sold in the two years following his death.<sup id="cite_ref-Lycett_(DNB)_1-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lycett_(DNB)-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> In 2008 <i>The Times</i> ranked Fleming fourteenth on its list of "The 50 greatest British writers since 1945".<sup id="cite_ref-205" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-205">&#91;200&#93;</a></sup> In 2002 Ian Fleming Publications announced the launch of the <a href="/wiki/CWA_Ian_Fleming_Steel_Dagger" title="CWA Ian Fleming Steel Dagger">CWA Ian Fleming Steel Dagger</a> award, presented by the <a href="/wiki/Crime_Writers%27_Association" title="Crime Writers&#39; Association">Crime Writers\' Association</a> to the best thriller, adventure or spy novel originally published in the UK.<sup id="cite_ref-Cork_Bookseller_206-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cork_Bookseller-206">&#91;201&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Eon Productions series of Bond films, which started in 1962 with <i>Dr. No</i>, continued after Fleming\'s death. Along with two non-Eon produced films, there have been twenty four Eon films, with the most recent, <i><a href="/wiki/Spectre_(2015_film)" title="Spectre (2015 film)">Spectre</a></i>, released in October 2015.<sup id="cite_ref-207" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-207">&#91;202&#93;</a></sup> The Eon Productions series has grossed over $6.2 billion worldwide, making it one of the highest-grossing film series.<sup id="cite_ref-208" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-208">&#91;203&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The influence of Bond in the cinema and in literature is evident in films and books including <a href="/wiki/Austin_Powers_(film_series)" class="mw-redirect" title="Austin Powers (film series)"><i>Austin Powers</i> series</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-209" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-209">&#91;204&#93;</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Carry_On_Spying" title="Carry On Spying">Carry On Spying</a></i><sup id="cite_ref-210" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-210">&#91;205&#93;</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Jason_Bourne" title="Jason Bourne">Jason Bourne</a> character.<sup id="cite_ref-Cork_Bookseller_206-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cork_Bookseller-206">&#91;201&#93;</a></sup> In 2011 Fleming became the first English-language writer to have an international airport named after him: <a href="/wiki/Ian_Fleming_International_Airport" title="Ian Fleming International Airport">Ian Fleming International Airport</a>, near <a href="/wiki/Oracabessa" title="Oracabessa">Oracabessa</a>, Jamaica, was officially opened on 12 January 2011 by Jamaican Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Bruce_Golding" title="Bruce Golding">Bruce Golding</a> and Fleming\'s niece, Lucy.<sup id="cite_ref-211" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-211">&#91;206&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Works">Works</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ian_Fleming&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Works">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_James_Bond_novels_and_short_stories" title="List of James Bond novels and short stories">List of James Bond novels and short stories</a></div>\n<div>\n\n&#32;\n<table class="multicol" role="presentation" style="border-collapse: collapse; padding: 0; href="#cite_note-225">&#91;k&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li><i><a href="/wiki/Octopussy_and_The_Living_Daylights" title="Octopussy and The Living Daylights">Octopussy and The Living Daylights</a></i> (1966)<sup id="cite_ref-227" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-227">&#91;l&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>\n<p>&#32;\n</p>\n</td></tr></tbody></table></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Biographical_films">Biographical films</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ian_Fleming&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Biographical films">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<ul><li><i>Goldeneye: The Secret Life of Ian Fleming</i>, 1989. A television film starring <a href="/wiki/Charles_Dance" title="Charles Dance">Charles Dance</a> as Fleming. The film focuses on Fleming\'s life during the Second World War, his love life and the writing of James Bond.<sup id="cite_ref-228" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-228">&#91;216&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li><i><a href="/wiki/Spymaker:_The_Secret_Life_of_Ian_Fleming" title="Spymaker: The Secret Life of Ian Fleming">Spymaker: The Secret Life of Ian Fleming</a></i>, 1990. A television film starring <a href="/wiki/Jason_Connery" title="Jason Connery">Jason Connery</a> (son of <a href="/wiki/Sean_Connery" title="Sean Connery">Sean</a>) as Fleming in a Bond-like adventure set during World War II.<sup id="cite_ref-229" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-229">&#91;217&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li><i><a href="/wiki/Ian_Fleming:_Bondmaker" title="Ian Fleming: Bondmaker">Ian Fleming: Bondmaker</a></i>, 2005. A television <a href="/wiki/Docudrama" title="Docudrama">drama-documentary</a>, first broadcast on <a href="/wiki/BBC" title="BBC">BBC</a> on 28 August 2005. <a href="/wiki/Ben_Daniels" title="Ben Daniels">Ben Daniels</a> portrayed Fleming.<sup id="cite_ref-230" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-230">&#91;218&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li><i><a href="/wiki/Ian_Fleming:_Where_Bond_Began" title="Ian Fleming: Where Bond Began">Ian Fleming: Where Bond Began</a></i>, 2008. Television documentary about the life of Ian Fleming, broadcast 19 October 2008 by the BBC. Presented by former <a href="/wiki/Bond_girl" title="Bond girl">Bond girl</a>, Joanna Lumley.<sup id="cite_ref-231" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-231">&#91;219&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li>The film <i><a href="/wiki/Age_of_Heroes_(film)" title="Age of Heroes (film)">Age of Heroes</a></i> is based on the exploits of 30 Commando; <a href="/wiki/James_D%27Arcy" title="James D&#39;Arcy">James D\'Arcy</a> played Fleming.<sup id="cite_ref-232" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-232">&#91;220&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li><i><a href="/wiki/Fleming:_The_Man_Who_Would_Be_Bond" title="Fleming: The Man Who Would Be Bond">Fleming: The Man Who Would Be Bond</a></i>, a <a href="/wiki/BBC_America" title="BBC America">BBC America</a> television four-episode mini-series, broadcast in January and February 2014, starring <a href="/wiki/Dominic_Cooper" title="Dominic Cooper">Dominic Cooper</a> in the title role.<sup id="cite_ref-233" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-233">&#91;221&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-234" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-234">&#91;222&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ian_Fleming&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Outline_of_James_Bond" title="Outline of James Bond">Outline of James Bond</a></li></ul>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes_and_references">Notes and references</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ian_Fleming&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Notes and references">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p><b>Notes</b>\n</p>\n<div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em; list-style-type: lower-alpha;">\n<ol class="references">\n<li id="cite_note-6"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-6">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Since 2000 Robert Fleming &amp; Co has been part of <a href="/wiki/JP_Morgan_Chase" class="mw-redirect" title="JP Morgan Chase">JP Morgan Chase</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-14"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-14">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The school was near to the estate of the Bond family, who could trace their ancestry to an Elizabethan spy named John Bond, and whose motto was <i>Non Sufficit Orbis</i>\xe2\x80\x94the world is not enough.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-20"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-20">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Some sources provide the name as "Monique Panchaud de Bottomes".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChancellor200517_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChancellor200517-19">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-133"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-133">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">When he was 38, Fleming smoked up to 70 cigarettes a day;<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELycett1996172_131-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELycett1996172-131">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup> he had been having them custom made at Morland of Grosvenor Street since the 1930s, and three gold bands on the filter were added during the war to mirror his naval commander\'s rank.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChancellor200570_132-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChancellor200570-132">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-150"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-150">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Despite the thaw, the Cold War became increasingly tense again shortly afterwards, with the <a href="/wiki/Bay_of_Pigs_Invasion" title="Bay of Pigs Invasion">Bay of Pigs Invasion</a>, the construction of the <a href="/wiki/Berlin_Wall" title="Berlin Wall">Berlin Wall</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Cuban_Missile_Crisis" title="Cuban Missile Crisis">Cuban Missile Crisis</a> all occurring in an eighteen-month period from April 1961 to November 1962.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlack200549\xe2\x80\x9350_149-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlack200549\xe2\x80\x9350-149">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-214"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-214">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The first US paperback edition of <i>Casino Royale</i> was re-titled <i>You Asked For It</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPfeifferWorrall1998203_212-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPfeifferWorrall1998203-212">&#91;207&#93;</a></sup> and Bond\'s name was changed to "Jimmy Bond".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenson19889_213-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenson19889-213">&#91;208&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-216"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-216">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"> First US paperback edition of <i>Moonraker</i> was re-titled <i>Too Hot to Handle</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenson198811\xe2\x80\x9312_215-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenson198811\xe2\x80\x9312-215">&#91;209&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-218"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-218">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"> Consisting of: "From a View to a Kill"; "For Your Eyes Only"; "Risico"; "Quantum of Solace" and "The Hildebrand Rarity".</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-219"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-219">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Due to a legal battle, the book\'s storyline is also credited to <a href="/wiki/Kevin_McClory" title="Kevin McClory">Kevin McClory</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jack_Whittingham" title="Jack Whittingham">Jack Whittingham</a>;<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELycett1996432_114-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELycett1996432-114">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> see <a href="/wiki/Thunderball_(novel)#Controversy" title="Thunderball (novel)">the controversy over <i>Thunderball</i></a>.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-222"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-222">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Fleming refused to allow a paperback edition to be published in the UK,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELycett1996402_220-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELycett1996402-220">&#91;211&#93;</a></sup> but one was published after his death.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELycett1996446_221-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELycett1996446-221">&#91;212&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-225"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-225">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">See <a href="/wiki/The_Man_with_the_Golden_Gun_(novel)#Background" title="The Man with the Golden Gun (novel)">the controversy over authorship</a>.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-227"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-227">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Originally published as two stories, "Octopussy" and "The Living Daylights"; modern editions now also contain "The Property of a Lady" and "007 in New York".<sup id="cite_ref-226" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-226">&#91;215&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n</ol></div>\n<p><b>References</b>\n</p>\n<div class="reflist columns references-column-width"
